Experimental Analysis and Finite Element 
Model of the Vibration Sensitivity in
Hearing Aid Microphones
Johan Schroll-Fleischer
Nikolaj Tulinius
June 2015
Master Thesis
==PAGE:2==
TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
Abstract
Department of Mechanial Engineering
Centre for Acoustic-Mechanical Micro Systems
Master of Science
Experimental Analysis and Finite Element Model of the Vibration Sensitivity in
Hearing Aid Microphones
by Johan Schroll-Fleischer and Nikolaj Tulinius
The present thesis aims to shed light on the vibration sensitivity of hearing aid micro-
phones, with the ongoing process of feedback reduction as motivation. Experimental
analyses are performed as per the microphone-on-rod method originally described by M.
KILLION, 1974, in [1]. In order to thoroughly investigate the method itself and select
a suitable rod design, three different experimental setups are used to collect; the sound
pressure radiation ﬁeld in proximity to the microphone, the displacement of the micro-
phone housing, and the approached isolated mechanically induced vibration response
of the microphone diaphragm, all during axial (normal to the diaphragm) excitation. A
5.0 cm conical rod design is ultimately chosen as it meets the criteria best compared to
other designs. Two microphones are modeled by the Finite Element Method in ANSYS®
Mechanical APDL, Release 15.0.7; the cyldindrical Sonion 8002, and the rectangular So-
nion 50GC31. The experimentally obtained displacements of the microphone are applied
to the ANSYS models and used to validate their diaphragm responses. The numerical
models are able to agree with tendencies of the experimental responses, but a certain
discrepancy in magnitude is evident. Possible reasons for the difference are discussed.
Finally the numerical models are simulated for controlled excitations not possible to
perform in an experimental environment. The cylindrical microphone is found to be
mostly affected by axial vibrations, while the rectangular microphone is almost equally
affected by axial vibrations and vibrations normal to the inlet.
==PAGE:3==
DANMARKS TEKNISKE UNIVERSITET
Resumé
Institut for Mekanisk Teknologi
Center for Akustisk-Mekaniske Mikrosystemer
Kandidat
Eksperimentel Undersøgelse og Finite Element Model af Vibrationsfølsomheden i
Mikrofoner til Høreapparater
af Johan Schroll-Fleischer og Nikolaj Tulinius
Den foreliggende afhandling har til formål at kaste lys over vibrationsfølsomheden af
mikrofoner i høreapparater, med den igangværende proces om reduktion af feedback
som motivation. Eksperimentelle analyser udføres efter mikrofon-på-stang metoden,
oprindeligt beskrevet af M. KILLION, 1974, i [1]. For at være i stand til at vælge et
passende design af stangkonstruktionen anvendes tre forskellige forsøgsopstillinger til
at; visualisere lydtryks udbredelsesfeltet i nærhed af mikrofonen, måle forskydningen
af mikrofonhuset og den tilnærmelsesvis isolerede mekanisk inducerede mikrofonmem-
brans respons, altsammen udført under aksial (normal til membranplanet) excitation. Et
5.0 cm konisk stangdesign er valgt, da det opfylder kriterierne bedst i forhold til andre
testede designs. To mikrofoner modeleres ved brug af Finite Element Metoden i AN-
SYS® Mechanical APDL, Release 15.0.7; den cylindriske Sonion 8002 og den ﬁrkantede
Sonion 50GC31. De eksperimentielt fundne mikrofonhusforskydnings data indsættes
i ANSYS modellerne og bruges til at validere de respektive membraners respons. De
numeriske modeller har kun været i stand til at matche tendenserne af de eksperimen-
tielle responser, men størrelsesmæssigt er værdierne ikke ens. Mulige årsager til de
fundne forskelle diskuteres. Endeligt er de numeriske modeller anvendt til at simulere
kontrollerede excitationer, som ikke er mulige at udføre i laboratoriet. Den cylindriske
mirkofon er mest påvirket af aksielle vibrationer, mens den ﬁrkantede mikrofon næsten
er påvirket i samme grad af aksielle vibrationer, som vibrationer normale ift. mundingen.

Acknowledgements
First, we would like to thank our main supervisor Professor Jakob Søndergaard Jensen
from DTU ELEKTRO, whose guidance and dedication kept us going in the right direction.
Jakob gave us weekly feedback, kept us on track, and made sure that the report and
synergy of it was sustained in our focus.
An equally big thanks goes to our external supervisor, Senior Acoustic Engineer Morten
Birkmose Søndergaard from GN-ReSound R&D A/S. We had a warm welcome at GN
and Morten showed great dedication helping us use the lab equipment, provide us with
datasheets, and understand acoustic phenomena, which we greatly appreciate.
We are grateful for Dr.Techn. Jon Juel Thomsen, lector Kim Lau Nielsen, and associate
professor Vicente Cutanda’s willingness to share theories and ideas guiding us towards
improved ﬁnite element models.
Research technician Tina Eriksen and tool maker Ulf Hansen from GN-ReSound were
always ready to help us produce a part or solder wires, we would like to thank them for
their assistance.
There are also many friends, dorm mates, and family members whom we would also like
to thank for their help, time, and patience.

==PAGE:16==
CHAPTER
ONE
INTRODUCTION
With the ongoing improvement of technology, hearing aid customers are continuously
expecting more and more from modern hearing aids. Apart from sound quality and long
battery life, functions like smartphone- and recently also smartwatch connect-ability,
multiple programs for different situations, comfortability, all while maintaining ease of
use, become important factors when competing on todays hearing aid market. One
factor which the customers are not (or should not be) aware of though, a factor which
in many ways limits the possibilities of the above mentioned criteria, is the vibroacoustic
stability.
Vibroacoustic stability is the main goal when designing hearing aid microphones. It
means that it is desirable to have as large an ampliﬁcation as possible, without feedback
(see Section 1.3) becoming audible to the user.
A microphone diaphragm can be excited in two ways, by acoustic sound pressure waves
or forced mechanical vibrations of the microphone housing it is mounted in. The present
thesis seeks to scope the channel of forced mechanical vibrations, using the microphone-
on-rod experiment method described in [1]. The microphone is excited by a shaker, but
since this produces an acoustic pressure ﬁeld that is picked up by the microphone, a rod
is introduced to give a distance between the shaker and the microphone. In turn, the
rod may bring new problems with it such as transverse vibrations, added weight, relative
phase angles, etc., making it challenging to control the exact mechanical vibrations that
are forcing the microphone.
In collaboration with Danish hearing aid manufacturer GN-ReSound, a thorough inves-
tigation of the microphone-on-rod experiment method is on the agenda with the aim
1
==PAGE:17==
Chapter 1. Introduction
of converging upon a rod-design that gives the most precise control of the vibrations
forcing the microphone as possible. With experiments it becomes possible to validate
the ﬁnite element (FE) models of the tested microphones in order to perform numerical
simulations of vibrations.
1.1
Objective
The main objective of the present thesis is to investigate the microphone sensitivity of
hearing aid microphones. This is achieved by examining how to maximize the direct
control of vibrations induced to a hearing aid microphone when performing microphone-
on-rod experiments. The experiments are used to validate FE models of the microphones,
utilizing the ANSYS® Mechanical APDL, Release 15.0.7 ﬁnite element software package.
The approach to the main objective is deﬁned by the sub-objectives listed below:
1. Converge upon a rod design that best meets the criteria of maximizing control of
the vibration-input.
2. Obtain data of how the microphone housing is excited in three-space, including
phase angles, by microphone-on-rod experiments.
3. Investigate how the center point of the microphone diaphragm responds axially by
microphone-on-rod experiments.
4. Assign the three-space excitation of the microphone housing as input in the numer-
ical models before comparing and validating them if possible.
5. Use numerical models to simulate isolated excitation cases of interest and show
the directions of excitation that the diaphragm is most sensitive to.
1.2
Hearing Aids
This section gives a short introduction to the conﬁguration of a typical hearing aid and
how it works.
A hearing aid is always comprised of: A microphone to convert the incoming sound into
electrical signals, a processor to increase the amplitude of the signal and re-balance it,
and a receiver1 to emit the ampliﬁed electrical signals as sound. Depending on type
1A miniature loudspeaker.
2
==PAGE:18==
Chapter 1. Introduction
and application, numerous other components may also exist, see for example Figure 1.1,
which shows a cross section of a common Behind The Ear (BTE) hearing aid from GN-
ReSound with its major details. The BTE hearing aid design holds the largest current
market share due to its ease of use, and because it is ideal for users with a mild to
profound hearing impairment, [2].
FIGURE 1.1: Interior of a BTE hearing aid from GN-ReSound.
1.3
Feedback
FIGURE 1.2: Feedback paths, [2].
If the gain in a hearing aid is set too high, it can
cause an audible effect called feedback. Feedback
is when the acoustic sound pressure waves and me-
chanical vibrations emitted from the receiver are
being captured by the microphone, re-ampliﬁed,
and emitted again. This is in a sense a loop of
noise, which presents itself as an uncomfortable
squealing to the user. To reduce the feedback, all
paths must be investigated and sought to be optimized by e.g. changing material type,
geometry, or maybe the types of contacts between parts. Sub-channels of feedback are
presented below with reference to Figure 1.2 (channels of interest to the present thesis
are marked with red), [2]:
3
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 1. Introduction
A. Acoustic feedback is emitted from and/or travels through:
(1) The vent.
(2) Imperfect sealing of the earmould.
(3) Imperfect sealing in the joints of the tubing of BTE-type hearing aids.
(4) Emission from the tubing walls of BTE-type hearing aids.
(–) Acoustic transmission in the hearing aid (not shown in Figure 1.2).
B. Mechanical feedback is emitted from and/or travels through:
(5) Structural emission from the bearings and shell of the hearing aid.
(5) Structural emission from the components of the hearing aid, namely the
receiver.
Acoustic feedback can be minimized by enclosing the ear canal such that the emitted
sound from the receiver stays within the ear. But in the pursuit of ever smaller hearing
aid designs, the problem of mechanical feedback has become more and more striking,
thus the motivation behind this project. Note that this thesis does not attempt to solve
the problem of feedback, but rather explore the ﬁrst steps of trying to understand how
a hearing aid microphone responds to isolated mechanically induced vibrations, and
perhaps give ideas on how to improve the vibroacoustic stability.
1.4
Hearing Aid Microphones
Being the focus of the thesis, the hearing aid microphones subject to the study are further
described in this section.
A microphone is basically a transducer, that converts the energy from acoustic pressure
into current and voltage. Pressure waves from an incident sound source enters the mi-
crophone through an inlet port, and attacks the upper side of a very thin diaphragm with
a metalized surface. Underneath the diaphragm, separated by a thin air gap, is a rigid
piece of metal called the backplate. The backplate is coated with a teﬂon material called
an electret, as it has a permanent electric charge. When the diaphragm is displaced to-
wards the electret by the sound pressure, a greater charge is induced. The charges are led
through the backplate, to a small ’in-house’ ampliﬁer (also known as a buffer ampliﬁer),
that amplify the small charges into larger electrical outputs actually processable by the
external ampliﬁer. The analogue electric outputs leave the microphone through wires
soldered to the bottom. The relationship between input and output of the microphone
4
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 1. Introduction
is completely linear, meaning that the waveforms of the two are assumed identical. The
correlation between in- and output magnitude is deﬁned as the microphone sensitivity.
The microphone sensitivity is a speciﬁc characteristic of any microphone, and is required
to process the outputs from it further. [8, p. 21-22].
The human voice has a frequency range of roughly [0.1; 4] kHz, see Figure 1.3, and the
hearing range of the average human ear typically spans over [0.02; 20] kHz. Hearing aid
microphones are developed to work in a bandwidth around and beyond the frequency
range of normal speech, that is [0.1; 10] kHz.
FIGURE 1.3: Frequency range of speech, hearing, and microphones respectively, [3].
1.4.1
Microphone Speciﬁcations
Two electret condenser microphone are treated in the present thesis, the Sonion 8002
and the Sonion 50GC31. Figure 1.4a shows a simple illustration annotated with its most
important dimensions (backplate and other interior features are omitted), of the Sonion
8002 microphone, which is referred to as the cylindrical microphone. Next to it is a
picture indicating the size, see Figure 1.4b. The datasheet is available in Appendix B.1.
Table 1.1 below lists all parameters of the cylindrical microphone for both numerical
and analytical calculations. Sonion 50GC31 similarly has its design and dimensions
presented in Figure 1.5 and Table 1.2 respectively, and is referred to as the rectangular
microphone. The datasheet is available in Appendix B.2.

Chapter 1. Introduction
1.5
Structure of the Thesis
Chapter one describes the motivation behind the project, the objective of the thesis,
and a few introductory descriptions relevant to the thesis. Chapter two reviews the
theory of membranes and applies it in order to validate numerical models of membranes.
Chapter three shows the experimental setups and tests performed to converge upon
the best rod design, which then is used to obtain the data used as basis to validate
the numerical models on. Chapter four holds the modeling, validation, and usage of
the microphone ﬁnite element models, along with convergence analyses and some FEM
theory. Chapter ﬁve rounds up all ﬁndings and discusses them thoroughly and critically,
before concluding remarks on the thesis are stated and put into perspective to the project.
When referring to the microphone diaphragm in physical terms it is denoted ’diaphragm’,
whereas it is denoted ’membrane’ in terms of mathematical and ﬁnite element modeling.
All ﬁeld quantities, such as the excitation amplitude of the shaker given in [V] in Sec-
tion 3.1.5 for example, are root mean square (RMS) values, unless otherwise stated in
the thesis.
Code-language speciﬁc text is written in typewriter font for distinguisability, for exam-
ple: “One may use the APDL-command K,1,0,0,0 in ANSYS to create a keypoint.”.
8
==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER
TWO
MATHEMATICAL MODELING OF THE MICROPHONE
DIAPHRAGMS
This chapter documents the numerical modeling of the microphone diaphragms, their
validations by analytical solutions, and how to produce and interpret results obtained
from them. The FE software package ANSYS® Mechanical APDL, Release 15.0.7 is used.
Note that this chapter examines microphone diaphragm well beyond the functional
recording frequency range of hearing aid microphones. This is done to provoke the ﬁrst
eigenfrequencies of the membranes so that proper validations can be performed.
Before venturing into using any ﬁnite element model, it is of major importance to acquire
insight and understand the dynamics of the problem. FE models can produce many re-
sults, but involve many assumptions and factors easily forgotten, often with no warnings
from the program. So before full scale models are created, more simpliﬁed, but similar
problems are analyzed and compared to analytical solutions. Here the microphones
are stripped down to their single most important parts, the diaphragms. Considering a
diaphragm as a mechanical membrane, mathematical expressions of eigenfrequencies,
mode shapes and forced vibration responses are obtained from the literature.
2.1
Analytical Review of Membranes
The following is based on the literature, [4, ch. 13] and [9, app. D.4]. The diaphragm,
being a two-dimensional sheet with a very small thickness relative to its plane area, held
just above a back-plate in the microphone housing by tension forces, is mechanically most
9
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2. Mathematical Modeling
accurately described as a homogeneous and perfectly ﬂexible membrane. A membrane
is essentially a string expanded into a second dimension, i.e. a very thin and stretchable
structure having negligible bending stiffness. It cannot take any compression forces, and
it only gets its stiffness from the applied pre-tension per unit boundary length P.
Let the plane the membrane spans over be the xy-plane, and an externally applied load,
described as the force f(x, y, t) per unit area, act normal to this plane in the z-direction.
Each inﬁnitesimally small sectional area, dxdy, of the continuous membrane, can be
considered to have only one degree of freedom (DOF), z-displacement, as displacements
in the x- and y-directions are assumed very small compared to the dimensions of the
membrane. The deﬂection of the membrane as a function of position and time is denoted
w(x, y, t).
Force equilibrium (after deformation) in the z-direction, is performed with reference to
the free body diagram (FBD) shown in Figure 2.1:
FIGURE 2.1: FBD of the forces acting on an inﬁnitesimally small membrane element,
[4, p. 422].
The inertia force on the element is given by
ρmem(x, y)tmem

2.1.1.1
Finite Element Model of the Rectangular Membrane
With the analytical results at hand, a simple membrane FE-model is created and validated.
The ANSYS-script for the rectangular membrane and its corresponding tree-diagram are
available in Appendix E.1.1.

Chapter 2. Mathematical Modeling
A rectangular area, with material parameters listed in Table 1.2, is modeled and meshed
as shown in Figure 2.4.
FIGURE 2.4: Mesh of the simpliﬁed rectangular membrane model.
For this problem, SHELL181 is the appropiate element type in ANSYS, as it is particu-
larly useful when modeling thin curved shell-structures (the membrane is curved once
deformed), and can also model membranes when the membrane option, KEYOPT(1)=1,
is set in ANSYS. The membrane is constrained in all translational DOF (recall that
membranes cannot take any bending) along its boundary length.
The pre-tension in the membrane is applied by lowering the temperature, T [◦C], in
ANSYS. The temperature expansion coefﬁcient for Polyethylene terephthalate (PET),
αmem, is set to 117 µm m−1 ◦C−1, [5]. It should not be physically interpreted as it is
only used to control how much T needs to be lowered in order to obtain the desired
pre-tension, and as neither T nor αmem inﬂuences the rest of the analysis they can be
set to any value that ﬁts. T can now be calculated on the basis of the chosen αmem. The
equations below are inspired by [10, p. 9–10].
The thermal strain is calculated as:
ε = αmem(Tref −T) ,
(2.22)
where Tref is the ambient temperature, here just set to 0 ◦C for simplicity. Assuming the
membrane stress-strain ratio behaves linear elastically, the strain is equal to
ε =
σ
Emem/(1 −νmem) ,
(2.23)

T = −P(1 −νmem)

(2.25)
Solving the model statically, with only the temperature difference above applied, yields
a uniform stress distribution in the x- and the y-directions of 3.47 MPa which is exactly
equal to σ evaluated by (2.24). This veriﬁes the trick of lowering the temperature.
However, complications involving unavoidable in-plane sinusoidal membrane tensions
arise when applying harmonic analysis in ANSYS, this issue is discussed in Section 4.1.4,
where a circumventing solution of a scaling factor is introduced. In this chapter it is
however not an issue as the applied arbitrary external pressure of f0 = 0.5 Pa is the
dominant excitation in the analyses.
With the pre-tension in order, the model is solved for eigenfrequencies and their corre-
sponding mode shapes. ANSYS is able to do this simply by switching analysis type from
static to modal analysis. The six lowest eigenfrequencies are listed and compared in

for the rectangular model, or as an axisymmetric line with element type SHELL208. The
mesh of the non-axisymmetric model is shown in Figure 2.8.

it is considered validated. In Figure 2.9, the same analysis is also performed for an ax-
isymmetric model in ANSYS (green curve). The axisymmetric model has the advantages
of being simpler and much faster to solve, which proves valuable in the further analyses
of the non-axisymmetric microphone models in Chapter 4. It does come with some
limitations though, as applied forces and boundary conditions need to be axisymmetric
as well, and the model is unable to simulate any mode shapes for n > 0.

==PAGE:37==
CHAPTER
THREE
EXPERIMENTAL ANALYSES
This chapter documents how and why the experimental setups are applied, the tests
performed, and discussions of the results obtained.
3.1
Experimental Setups
Inspired by [1], the experiments are conducted using a test microphone mounted on
a rod, that is mounted on a shaker. In order to fulﬁll sub-objectives 1-3 in Section 1.1,
three different test setups are chosen in the laboratory:
• The Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry (LDV) setup (see Section 3.1.1) is used
to measure the dynamic response of the microphone housing during microphone-
on-rod experiments. These experiments help evaluate how purely the axial excita-
tion of the shaker is applied to the microphone housing. It is also later used as the
direct input that is applied to the FE models of microphones in Chapter 4.
• The Acoustic Holography setup (see Section 3.1.2) is useful to measure, plot,
and evaluate the sound ﬁeld radiation, as pressure waves are emitted from the
shaker, rod, and microphone for different excitation frequencies. The results serve
as indicators of the performance of different rod designs.
• The Anechoic Chamber setup (see Section 3.1.3) gives the response of the di-
aphragm relative to the microphone housing. In this setup the electrical output
of the mechanically excited microphone is measured, all while a ﬁxed control mi-
crophone is situated nearby, so the two can be subtracted in order to approach an
22
==PAGE:38==
Chapter 3. Experimental Analysis
isolation of the mechanical vibration response of the diaphragm. The idea is to
obtain something like the arbitrarily illustrated graph in Figure 3.1. These tests are
ultimately used to validate the ﬁnite element models in Chapter 4.
FIGURE 3.1: Arbitrary illustration of what a ﬁnal validation plot may look like.
3.1.1
Scanning Laser Doppler Vibrometry
The LDV utilizes non-contact laser technology to measure target point amplitude in the
axial direction of the laser. Outputs are obtained as complex numbers where modulus is
the measured velocity amplitude and argument is the phase angle.
From the amplitude of the cyclic velocity, the displacement- and acceleration amplitudes
can be obtained by dividing or multiplying with 2πΩHz respectively. This makes it ideal
to measure the microphone or shaker dynamics as a function of excitation frequency.
The LDV is unable to measure the diaphragm movement directly however, as the laser
shoots straight through it1. Figure 3.2 shows the major details of the LDV setup.
FIGURE 3.2: Sketch of the scanning LDV test setup.
1M. Søndergaard, GN-ReSound R&D A/S
23
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 3. Experimental Analysis
The LDV head can be tilted 90°, enabling it to capture the displacement of each direction
by moving it around the test specimen. The measuring tolerances of the laser are well
within the operational range to deliver usable results, Appendix B.3.
An example of a Matlab script, written and used to import, process and plot LDV data, is
available in Appendix D.1.
3.1.2
Acoustic Holography
In the acoustic holography tests, the shaker, rod, and microphone are placed on an
aluminum plate surrounded by absorbing foam, see Figure 3.3. While inducing vibrations
through the shaker, an intensimetric probe travels through a pre-speciﬁed grid of points,
and collects independent measurements of intensity and pressure. After processing in
the computer, the system yields data that is then exported or plotted directly to show the
sound radiation ﬁeld near the source.
FIGURE 3.3: Sketch of the holography setup.
All holography tests are performed with an 8 × 8 cm grid with a 0.5 cm spacing between
each point.
An example of a Matlab script, written and used to import, process and plot acoustic
holography data, is available in Appendix D.2.
24
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 3. Experimental Analysis
3.1.3
Anechoic Chamber
The anechoic chamber is a container of sorts, used to reduce all external sounds when
testing the diaphragm response as a function of shaker excitation frequency. A control
microphone is, via a small arm and blue-tack, held as close to the shaken microphone as
possible. Figure 3.4 shows a sketch of the setup.
FIGURE 3.4: Sketch of the anechoic chamber setup.
The microphone outputs are all obtained in amplitudes of [dB V] and phase angles φ [°].
To convert this data into units of decibel sound pressure level Lp [dB SPL], the micro-
phone sensitivity of smic = −33.5 dB V/Pa, (assumed constant in the range [0.1; 10] kHz,
from Appendix B.1) is used with a reference decibel sound pressure level of 94 dB SPL =
1 Pa:
−33.5 dB V/Pa = 1 dB SPL
⇒
−33.5 dB V = 94 dB SPL .
(3.1)
An example of a Matlab script, written and used to import, process and plot anechoic
chamber data, is available in Appendix D.3.
3.1.4
Rod Designs
The design of the rod holds an interesting dilemma as it must be long enough to render
acoustic pressure waves originating from the shaker’s membrane negligible, but it must
25
==PAGE:41==
Chapter 3. Experimental Analysis
also be rigid enough to assume that the input from the shaker is applied only in the axial
direction, and no signiﬁcant movement occurs in the transverse directions. The top of
the rod must also have the same diameter as the microphone to avoid any sharp edges,
possibly causing additional sound radiation near the microphone.
Figure 3.5a shows the design drawing of the ﬁrst three rods, of respectively 5.0, 10.0
and 15.0 cm lengths. The plastic disc is needed because the microphone has three wires
soldered to the bottom of the housing, which would otherwise short-circuit if mounted
directly on the steel rod.
(A) Drawing of the ﬁrst rod designs.
(B) Drawing of the conical rod designs.
FIGURE 3.5: Steel rod design sketches.
The rods illustrated in Figure 3.5a are referred to as 5.0, 10.0 and 15.0 cm rods respec-
tively, when showing and discussing experimental results. After some tests, the need for
stiffer rods became present, and another rod design shown in Figure 3.5b is proposed.
In experiments these rods are referred to as 5.0, 7.5 and 10.0 cm conical rods. Later on,
yet another non-conical 2.5 cm rod is introduced, and referred to as 2.5 cm rod in the
experiments.
Note that the coordinate system depicted in Figures 3.5a and 3.5b is used as the reference
frame throughout the rest of the thesis.
26
==PAGE:42==
Chapter 3. Experimental Analysis

The ampliﬁer for the shaker has a set amplitude of 0.5 V speciﬁed throughout all experi-
ments and the excitation frequency range is set to [0.1; 10] kHz as standard. This range
is chosen because it is the frequency range of a typical hearing aid, see Appendices B.1
and B.2.
3.2
Experiments and Results
Series of tests are performed to obtain the data necessary to choose the best rod design,
and validate the numerical models. Each are described and discussed in this section.
3.2.1
Testing the Correlation Between Transverse and Axial Amplitudes
The assumption that the axial vibrational amplitudes are dominant in the experiments
is critical. Numerical simulations become more complicated and harder to verify if vi-
brations in multiple directions must be included. The transverse amplitudes, unlike the
axial, are not directly controllable, and are harder to measure precisely, since the rod is
screwed in with at least a slight difference each time, and slightly pivoted between mea-
surements. To test this assumption, the axial and transverse amplitudes are measured
and plotted for the 5.0, 10.0 and 15.0 cm rods respectively in Figure 3.6, and for the 2.5
rod, and the 5.0, and 10.0 cm conical rods in Figure 3.7

29
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 3. Experimental Analysis
The transverse amplitudes are far from negligible, for some excitation frequencies they
even overshoot the axial amplitudes. There can be multiple reasons for this: Natural
frequencies of the rod, angling of the shaker, effects of adhesives, or perhaps just trans-
verse vibrations of the shaker base. Whether the 5.0 cm or the 10.0 cm rod is better in
terms of lowest transverse vibration amplitudes is hard to tell, as they have very different
transverse patterns, but the overall magnitude seems the same. The 15.0 cm rod shows
an eigenfrequency at Ω≈9 kHz, affecting both the axial- and transverse amplitudes of
the response, which is undesirable. The plots exclude the option that the shaker base
is angled, as that should be clearly visible in transverse amplitude differences between
each rod length. The conical rods yield smaller transverse amplitudes than their same
length counterparts, however the difference is not signiﬁcant. The 5.0 cm conical rod is
able to compete with the 2.5 cm rod at least for low excitation frequencies.
3.2.2
Bending Eigenfrequencies of the Rods
To ﬁnd out if the rod eigenfrequencies coincide with the axial or transverse response
peaks found experimentally, the rods are simpliﬁed to mechanical cantilever beams
(the thick part and the thread are assumed an inﬁnitely stiff wall) with a circular cross
section (the eigenfrequencies of the conical rods are not reviewed). The bending mode
eigenfrequencies of such are simple to calculate using a formula from [9, App. (D.4)]:

When comparing Table 3.2 and Figure 3.6, peaks and eigenfrequency values are recog-
nizable, but they are not all precise and some cannot be found in the plots. The reason
for this is related to faults introduced by the analytical assumptions not being met, e.g.
imperfect rod-shaker clamping. Some of the peaks can also be due to the eigenfrequen-
cies of the shaker. With the equipment currently available, it does not seem possible to
attain better solutions, it is simply noted that the rods do have eigenfrequencies within
the excitation range, that some of them are recognizable in the table above, and that
shorter and stiffer rods yield less of them in the frequency range of interest.
3.2.3
Testing the Effects of the Adhesive Assembly Materials
The adhesives between rod, plastic, and microphone can possibly cause phase angle lags
due to viscous effects. The tests in this section are performed to review their signiﬁcance.
The plastic part is glued to the rod, and the microphone is ﬁxed to the plastic with
beeswax. In the tests, the 5.0, 10.0, and 15 cm rods each have their transverse vibration
amplitudes measured. Three points for each rod are recorded: the top of the steel rod,
the center of the plastic part, and the center of the microphone steel housing (from the
side). The results are plotted in Figure 3.8.

From the amplitude plots there is seemingly a noticeable difference between rod, plastic,
and microphone, which tends to increase for higher excitation frequencies. Theoretically
this makes sense, because higher excitation frequencies excite higher order modes, and
with higher order modes, the slope θ(z) of the deformed rod becomes steeper, increasing
the difference between adjacent points. To be able to neglect the effects of the adhesives,
the theory above is tested by a calculation of the deﬂection of a simple cantilever beam
([9, Table D.3]), see Figure 3.9a.

3.2.5
Testing the Direction of Transverse Vibrations
When performing transverse vibration measurements, the pivot angle of the shaker and
the amount the rod is screwed into the shaker are unlikely to be the same. Meaning that
unless the transverse motion of the rod is perfectly circular in the xy-plane, there is no
guarantee whether it is actually the x- or the y-component or a mix that is measured in
between each test.
To test the difference of transverse response in the x- vs. y directions, three measure-
ments of the transverse response of the conical 5.0 cm rod, where the shaker is turned 0,
45, and 90° around the z-axis respectively, are plotted in Figure 3.11.

The purple curve represents the resulting transverse magnitude from the 0° (blue) and 90°

there are no changes in transverse amplitude in between LDV tests is required. This is
closest achieved by sampling data from the different axes rapidly after each other, while
making sure that the rod and shaker stays exactly the same throughout the experiments.
In conclusion, the transverse amplitudes are differentiating in the x- and y directions.
Therefore, when the numerical microphone models are to be solved, different inputs in x-
and y (the measured responses) must be applied. The former tests using the transverse
amplitudes in an unknown direction are not reworked, since the data just serves as a
reference to review the dominance of the axial vibrations in Section 3.2.1.
3.2.6
Acoustic Holography Measurements
While LDV measurements concern the mechanical response of the microphone, acoustic
holography experiments concern vibro-acoustic radiation from acoustic sources, e.g. the
shaker and the rod. The results are based on three input variables: x-location, z-location
(see Figure 3.5a for coordinate axes), and excitation frequency Ω. With so many inputs,
output data quickly becomes overwhelmingly difﬁcult to manage, so in the two plots
below, denoted waterfall-plots, the x-variable is set to 0 m, and a 3D plot is visualized
with Lp as a function of distance from the shaker (z-location) and excitation frequency.
Multiple measurements are done, but here only the 5 cm rod and the 5 cm conical rod
waterfall-plots are shown to provide data examples, see Figures 3.12 and 3.13.

3.2.7
Anechoic Chamber Measurements
LDV and acoustic holography measurements both concern the external effects from
the microphone on the rod, while the anechoic chamber measurements are focused
on the internal diaphragm response. This section presents the approximately isolated
mechanically induced excitation responses of the diaphragm for different rod designs,
microphone geometries, and mounting angles.
The experiments yield two measurement results, one for the shaken microphone, and
one for the control microphone, both in units of [dB V]. Using (3.1), the units are
converted to [dB SPL], and an example of the cylindrical microphone mounted upright
on the 5.0 cm conical rod is presented in Figure 3.16.

phase angles of each are easily taken into account when subtracted from one another.
One may argue that since the two microphones are not coinciding with each other, the
sound radiation ﬁeld must differ at each microphone. But since the sound differentiation
mainly happens vertically (see Figure 3.15), while the microphones are positioned in
the same height and the minimum wavelength λ = 30 mm is large compared to the
microphone position difference of 5 mm, the sound pressure at the two microphone
positions are assumed to have the same phase. A close-up schematic of the setup is
available in Figure 3.17.

FIGURE 3.17: The size of the minimum wavelength renders the position difference
between the two microphones to be negligible.
The observant reader may wonder why there is such a large difference between the
holography results in Figure 3.14 and the anechoic chamber results in Figure 3.16. The
reason for this is that the holography results average over a set of values/frequencies,
making them lower than the directly measured tests from the control microphone in the
anechoic chamber measurements. The control microphone is therefore the reliable one,
again implying that the holography results should be interpreted as tendencies rather
than actual magnitudes.

The best rod can not be found simply as the one yielding the largest response in the two
plots above. The largest response means that the mechanical vibrations dominate more,
but as proven earlier, the magnitude of transverse vibration amplitudes is also increased
for longer rod lengths (see Section 3.2.2), causing the dominant mechanical vibrations
to come from undesired directions. Therefore the best rod design must be found by an
overall comparison of all the tests performed.

3.2.7.3
Angled Microphones
Assuming that vibrations normal to the diaphragm dominate the response of it, the
response obtained from a horizontally lying microphone should be much lower (as long
as transverse excitations are not dominant). To test this, the rectangular and cylindrical
microphones are angled 90°, fastened to the 5.0 cm conical rod, and tested in the anechoic
chamber. Figure 3.21 shows the results.

vibrations normal to the diaphragm are dominant.
3.3
Evaluation of the Experiments
More than half the experiments described in this chapter are made in regards to the
possible errors of assumptions and mapping the signiﬁcance of them. In some of the
tests the errors are proven to be signiﬁcant with no countermeasure available. So even
with much effort and caution put into experiments and data treatment, the results may
sometimes be inaccurate. The objectives of the experiments in this chapter are to con-
verge upon a rod design that eliminates as many sources of errors as possible, so that
results can be obtained as controlled as possible. The fact of the matter is that the test
setup study easily can be expanded into a full size project on its own, where many more
rod designs, shakers, and microphone mounting solutions can be investigated.
The 5.0 cm conical rod, see Figure 3.5b, is ultimately chosen as it is the rod that best
fulﬁlls the criteria stated in Section 3.1.4. This rod design performs well in regards to
axial vs. transverse vibrations up to Ω≲2 kHz, see Figure 3.7b. From Figure 3.14,
the large weight of the rod results in low sound pressure emissions from the shaker,
compared to other designs, which is desirable. From Section 3.2.7, this rod design has a
good performance, because it is imperative that mechanical vibrations are the dominant
source of the diaphragm response compared to acoustic pressure.
45

FINITE ELEMENT MODELING OF

With the results from Chapter 3 at hand, the numerical models of the microphones are
now created in ANSYS in an attempt to validate them against experimental results.
The microphones described in Section 1.4.1 are modeled in ANSYS individually as com-
bined structural-acoustic systems.
The intention is to calculate the response of the
diaphragm when conducting harmonic analysis. The cyclic displacements are applied to
the microphone housing (rod and adhesives are omitted), so that it is displaced (in the
transverse xy-plane and axial z-direction) with microphone housing motion data from
LDV experiments including phase angles. The displacement amplitudes and phase angles
shown in Figure 4.1 are directly converted from the velocities measured in Figure 3.11.

CAD-models of the Sonion 8002- and 50GC31 electret

and 4.3 as renderings in Creo® Parametric 2.0, and
used as references to build the geometries. It is possi-

ric details that are futile to the ﬁnite element analysis
(FEA) would nonetheless complicate meshing and in-
crease computational time. A simple geometry, built
from scratch in ANSYS, also enables a higher level of
control of the model and makes it easier to interpret
results from it. The model geometry is thus reduced to
the fewest most important parts: the diaphragm, and
the microphone housing. When doing so, mirror- and
even axisymmetry can be exploited, thus the models in ANSYS are created as illustrated
in Figures 1.4a and 1.5a, using the values listed in Tables 1.1 and 1.2.
FIGURE 4.3: CAD Sonion 50GC31
microphone model.
Mirror- and axisymmetric models reduce the com-
putational time, but cannot model transverse vibra-
tions in the x- and xy-directions respectively. In or-
der to have both an efﬁcient model, and one able to
take excitation inputs in all axes, an axisymmetric,
mirror-symmetric, and a full microphone model are
all built. Similarly the rectangular microphone is mod-
eled both as a full- and mirror-symmetric model. For
an overview, see Figure E.1 in Appendix E.2.
Throughout this chapter many model tests are performed up to Ω= 20 kHz, rather than
the operation range of the microphones, this is to provoke the ﬁrst eigenfrequency of the
diaphragm, making it safer to validate the model.
4.1
FEA of a Structure in a Fluid Medium
Before venturing into the creation, analysis, and results of the models, a short theory
section is provided to discuss some of the challenges encountered when analyzing a
microphone in air.
47
==PAGE:63==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
4.1.1
Fluid-Structure Interaction (FSI)
When a structure is displaced into an enclosed ﬂuid, the ﬂuid responds with an increase of
pressure exertion on its container. Meaning that the increase in pressure not only affects
all of the contained ﬂuid hydrostatically, but also acts as a distributed force resisting the
displacement of the structure.
To model this mathematically, the well known formulation of structural displacement in
ﬁnite element analysis is used as a basis:
[M] { ¨D} + [C] { ˙D} + [K] {D} = {R} ,
(4.1)
where [M] is the mass matrix, { ¨D} is the accelerations vector, [C] is the damping matrix,
{ ˙D} is the velocity vector, [K] is the stiffness matrix, {D} is the displacement vector, and
{R} is the force vector.
In relevance to the present thesis, the structure is exposed to time-varying displace-
ments, in turn causing time-varying nodal loads on the ﬂuids, {RF}, with a negative
sign for equilibrium as shown in the acoustic formulation, from [13, eq. (12.7-20)]:
[MF] {¨P} + [CF] { ˙P} + [KF] {P} = −{RF} ,
(4.2)
where [MF] is the ﬂuid mass matrix, {¨P} is the second time derivative of the ﬂuid
pressure vector, [CF] is the ﬂuid damping matrix, { ˙P} is the ﬁrst time derivative of the
ﬂuid pressure vector, [KF] is the ﬂuid stiffness matrix, and {P} is the ﬂuid pressure
vector. The load from the structure acting on the ﬂuid is {RF}.
The coupled acoustic ﬂuid-structural system, with an asymmetric matrix equation, from
[13, eq. (12.8-6)], then becomes:
"
[M]
[0]
ρ [S]
[MF ]
# (
{ ¨D}
{¨P}
)
+
"
[C]
[0]
[0]
[CF ]
# (
{ ˙D}
{ ˙P}
)
+
"

−[S]T
[0]
[KF ]

(4.3)
where ρ is the density of the ﬂuid and [S] is an integration of a product of shape functions
over the interaction surface, [13, eq. (12.8-2)]. The product ρ [S] { ¨D}, which is equal to
{RF}, from (4.2). {Rext} is a part of the nodal loads vector {R}, shown in (4.1), where
{R} = {Rext} + [S]T {P}. The part, [S]T {P}, is thus the load from the ﬂuid acting on
the structure.
48
==PAGE:64==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
FIGURE 4.4: FSI.
In ANSYS, the FSI is modeled by use of acoustic ele-
ments with KEYOPT(2)=0, meaning that the nodes, in
addition to pressure, also include spatial DOF. As il-
lustrated in Figure 4.4, the FSI-nodes are made up of
shared nodes between structural and ﬂuid elements.
4.1.2
Double Layered Membrane
As shell elements are created only as a line (axisymmetric) or an area (3D), they do
not respond correctly when having ﬂuids on both sides. This is because the shared FSI
nodes have pressure DOF on both sides, allowing pressure to be directly transferred
between the two sides without interacting with the diaphragm. To compensate for this,
two coincident layers of shell elements (referred to as layers A and B) are created. The
FSI ﬂag from the top air, is assigned to the nodes on layer A, while the bottom air FSI
ﬂag is assigned to the nodes on layer B. The displacement DOF of the nodes on layers
A and B are then coupled. For the two-layer diaphragm to have the same material
characteristics as a one-layer diaphragm, the E-moduli and densities of both layers must
be half of the resulting parameters while all others remain the same, [14, p. 5]:
EA = EB = E
2 ,
(4.4)
ρA = ρB = ρ
2 ,
(4.5)
νA = νB = ν ,
(4.6)
tmemA = tmemB = tmem ,
(4.7)
αA = αB = α .
(4.8)
With basis on the numerical frequency response for the non- and axisymmetric circular
membranes plotted in Figure 2.9, the simulation has been repeated with two coincident
layers modeled as described above, yielding the comparison plot in Figure 4.5.

FIGURE 4.5: Numerical frequency response for the non- and axisymmetric circular, and

The plots are coincident and thus the double-layered membrane method is validated and
will be used in all models.
4.1.3
Ambient Air Bubble
Since the acoustics problem in the present thesis is of the exterior kind, involving an
inﬁnite homogeneous inviscid ﬂuid, the surrounding air must be modeled to attain
negligible pressure reﬂection in the boundaries.
FIGURE 4.6: Bubble drawing.
This is achieved by forming the air as a spherical enclosure
with the microphone model in the center, and radius ra.
A sphere maximizes the chance that the pressure waves,
originating from the model, attack the boundary perpen-
dicularly, see Figure 4.6. If an attacking pressure wave has
a tangential component, that part of the wave is reﬂected
back into the air media, which contaminates the response.
In theory, ra should ideally be inﬁnitely long, which is of
course not possible by the ﬁnite element method. A practical size of ra is, from [15]:
ra ≥1
5λ = 1
5
c
ΩHz
[m] ,
(4.9)
where c

m s−1
is the speed of sound in the ﬂuid medium.
50
==PAGE:66==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
More conservatively, the Abaqus help manual, [16], recommends a minimum of
ra ≥1
3λ ,
(4.10)
which is in good agreement with (4.9). The more conservative choice from Abaqus
is used as basis in the present thesis. The size of the ra is thus further explored by a
convergence study in Section 4.2.2.
4.1.4
Scaling Factor Convergence Analysis
Much effort has been done to overcome what seems to be an unavoidable limitation of
ANSYS. The static in-plane pre-tension of the membrane (by a temperature difference
introduced in Section 2.1.1.1) also becomes sinusoidally cyclic, with a zero phase angle,
when performing harmonic analysis in ANSYS, (see [15, PSTRES Notes]). The fact
that the membrane cycles between stiff and loose states during analyses is indeed not
resembling reality, but proven negligible in Section 2.1.1.1 due to a dominant pressure,
see Figure 2.5. But when the membrane excitation becomes sufﬁciently small, which
some of the displacements from the LDV-tests are, the unwanted cyclic pre-stress begins
to contaminate the results. To compensate for this, a scaling factor Sd on the amplitude
of excitation is introduced. This requires an underlying assumption that the problem is
linear, which is already an assumption from harmonic FEA in the ﬁrst place.
To ﬁnd out what value of the scaling factor, Sd, makes the external displacement exci-
tation of the microphone dominant compared to the unwanted cyclic pre-tension, the
frequency response of the axisymmetric microphone model (Sonion 8002) (see Sec-
tion 4.2) is obtained for varied axial excitation amplitudes:
dAmp = ¯dAmp × Sd
for
Sd = {1, 10, 100, 1000, 10000} ,
(4.11)
where ¯dAmp = 8.1758 × 10−10 m (the average of the axial displacement from the LDV
tests in Figure 4.1 when neglecting the very high amplitudes from the beginning), the
middle-node of the membrane and a bottom-node of the microphone housing are the
points of interest when attaining results. The amplitude parts of the results are divided
by their respective scaling factors and plotted in Figure 4.7.
51

Convergence is reached for a scaling factor Sd ≳100. Without the use of a scaling factor
(blue curves) it is clear that the simulation is too contaminated by the effects of the
unwanted cyclic nature of the pre-tension and it does not produce correct results.
4.1.5
From Displacement to Sound Pressure Level
The diaphragm voltage output data can be converted to pressure, but when performing
analyses in ANSYS the membrane output is obtainable only in terms of displacement. To
make the two units comparable, a converter is needed. This section describes how a con-
verter is artiﬁcially created using an analytical approach, as well as with the axisymmetric
cylindrical microphone model in ANSYS, see Section 4.2.
The idea behind both methods is to solve the static membrane center deﬂection problem
for a number of different uniform pressures applied to it. This requires the assump-
tion that an incoming pressure wave distributes as a uniform pressure normal to the
diaphragm surface, this assumption is reviewed in Appendix A.4 where it is also vali-
dated.
Using (2.33) and (3.7), the analytical membrane deﬂection as a function of Lp can be
computed. In ANSYS the membrane is solved for a direct pressure application, and in
another test for a pressure increase in the air nodes near the membrane (thus resembling
incoming sound pressure more realistically), but where the air behind the membrane is
removed to avoid it giving a stiffening effect. All tests yield similar results as shown in
Figure 4.8.
52
==PAGE:68==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
Lp [dB SPL]
0
20
40
60
80
100
120
140
z [m]

10-11
10-10
10-9
10-8
10-7
10-6
10-5
Circular Analytical
Rectangular Analytical
Direct pres. ANSYS
Incident air pres. ANSYS
FIGURE 4.8: Static membrane center displacements as a function of applied sound
pressure.
The conversion plot above must be used with caution as it is created in an artiﬁcial sense
from the analytical formulas and numerical ANSYS models. If possible, it is much more
safe to use one obtained from experimental methods.
For Lp < 60 dB SPL the results from ANSYS bend away from their initial path, because
for pressure levels that low the displacement from the cyclic pre-tension of the membrane
begins to dominate, giving a good example of the necessity of Sd. Based on the analytical
results, an exponential function (see (4.12)) is ﬁtted to describe the static displacement
of the center of the diaphragm as a function of Lp:
z [m] = bcyl · aLp [dB SPL]
cyl
,
(4.12)
where acyl = 1.124 and bcyl = 9.952 × 10−13 have been calculated by solving two equa-
tions with two unknowns for two arbitrarily chosen points from the analytical results
(black line), in Figure 4.8, of the circular model. The rectangular model coefﬁcients
are calculated to arect = 1.122 and brect = 1.484 × 10−12. With these coefﬁcients set,
any diaphragm displacement results from the simulations can be converted into units of
[dB SPL] using (4.12).
4.2
Axisymmetric Model (Sonion 8002)
This section describes the modeling, validation and result-processing associated with the
axisymmetric ANSYS model of the Sonion 8002 microphone. The ANSYS script written
and used to obtain analytical results in this section is available in Appendix E.2.1.
53
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
The microphone housing is created on basis of the data listed in Table 1.1, and meshed
using the 4-node element type PLANE182. The membrane is modeled by the same means,
as validated in Section 2.1.2.1, with application of the two layer membrane method,
as shown and also validated, in Section 4.1.2. The diaphragm is thus created as two
lines, and meshed with the 2-node axisymmetric element type SHELL208. The air is
meshed with the 4-node element type FLUID29, and the inﬁnite medium with a 2-node
line element type on top of the enclosing circle edge with the absorbing element type
FLUID129. All element types are called with the KEYOPT(3)=1 command to make them
axisymmetric.
Figure 4.9 shows a plot giving an overview of the axisymmetric model.
FIGURE 4.9: Illustration of geometry and element types of the axisymmetric model.
Low order element types are used, as no higher order ﬂuid element types are available in
2D. When meshing it is imperative that all ﬂuid-structure coupling nodes coincide, thus
involving higher order elements would mess up the interface. Certain tricks, [17], can be
applied to circumvent the issue, but is deemed an unnecessary effort in the present thesis.
Thus, p-reﬁnement of the mesh, as described in [13, p. 318-319], is not performed in
this project.
54
==PAGE:70==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
4.2.1
Axisymmetric Mesh Convergence Analysis
To be able to trust results from the axisymmetric model, a mesh convergence analysis
is performed. Solving the model for an increasingly ﬁner mesh, also known as an h-
reﬁnement (see [13, p. 318-319]), helps identify whether the solution is sufﬁciently
accurate as well as the sign of the error.
The element side lengths are controlled by a gridsize variable. All elements are controlled
by this variable, although some of them are multiplied by a factor when they are near
uninteresting edges. Initially, an arbitrary length of gridsize = hfair/2 = 3.5 × 10−5 m
is set. The model is then solved for a prescribed axial displacement with an amplitude,
¯dAmp = 8.1758 × 10−10 m, multiplied by a scaling factor of Sd = 100.
Figure 4.10 shows a plot of how the membrane center displacement develops with de-
and increasing mesh reﬁnements for three different excitation frequencies.

4.2.2
Ambient Air Bubble Radius Convergence Analysis
Equation (4.10) yields ra = 113 mm, for Ω= 1 kHz, which is ∼40 times as large as
the microphone height. From (4.10), the bubble size increases for smaller values of Ω,
and if the most conservative solution should be taken, the bubble becomes enormous
in size compared to the microphone. This creates considerable problems with the mesh
generation, when both the element aspect ratio, microphone mesh reﬁnement, and calcu-
lation times need to be within reasonable limits. So to test the signiﬁcance of the bubble
size, the membrane center displacement as a function of Ωand bubble size is plotted in
Figure 4.12. A value of 100 % refers to a bubble size that suits a benchmark excitation
frequency of Ω= 5 kHz in order to provoke incorrect values at lower frequencies. The
bubble size is then reduced, and the model is recalculated.

57
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
The blue curve in Figure 4.13 represents the experimental results from the cylindrical
microphone on the 5.0 cm conical rod. The red curve is the direct membrane center
displacement amplitude from ANSYS, which is an output mainly used for convergence
analyses in this chapter. And the yellow curve is the membrane center displacement
relative to the microphone housing displacement, also including the phase difference.
As expected, the direct membrane displacements are very similar to the axially forced
displacements shown in Figure 4.1. The experiments and relative displacement of the
membrane center point shows the same tendencies. However, there is a persistent differ-
ence of ∼35 dB SPL present. One or more of the following errors may be responsible:
• The pre-tension value P = 5.2 N m−1 from Table 1.1 may be incorrect.
GN-
ReSound doubt its reliability as the microphone manufacturer supplied them with
different values.
• The assumption to neglect the backplate can cause incorrect diaphragm responses
for higher values of Ω, [18, p. 72-74]. When excluding the backplate, an air gap in
the order of ∼20 µm is also removed from the model. Normally the ﬂuid-structure
boundary layer in air (also in the same order of ∼20 µm), is negligible, due to its
diminutive thickness compared to the rest of the air medium. But since the air gap
between the backplate and diaphragm is so thin, the thermal and viscous boundary
layer dampening will affect the diaphragm motion. Microphone manufacturers
exploit this effect, and design the backplate to dampen the diaphragm response
near the ﬁrst eigenfrequency, which is why the error is most evident for higher
values of Ω.
• The artiﬁcially created converter is veriﬁed analytically, but may be based on some
crude assumptions.
• The axisymmetric model cannot take transverse displacements into account, but as
mentioned in Chapter 3, it should be possible to validate it at least for Ω≲2 kHz
nonetheless.
4.3
Cylindrical 3D Model (Sonion 8002)
This section brieﬂy describes the FE modeling of the cylindrical Sonion 8002 microphone
in 3D, after which it is validated and used for simulations.
The 3D geometry is built by extruding circular areas into cylindrical volumes, before
meshing each as air or structure. The diaphragm is modeled by meshing two of the
58
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
volume areas, and the ambient air, by subtracting all other volumes from a spherical
volume.
The 3D model is in many ways similar to the axisymmetric model, thus the element
types used in it are the third dimension equivalents to those used in the 2D axisymmetric
model. For an overview, the element types are listed in Table 4.1 below.

4.4
Rectangular 3D Model (Sonion 50GC31)
The rectangular Sonion 50GC31 is in many ways similar to the cylindrical microphone,
from Section 4.3, in terms of FE modeling in ANSYS. Thus, the rectangular microphone
is not as thoroughly documented or validated in this section.
The geometry is created by subtracting blocks from blocks, all sized after Table 1.2. The
element types for structure, membrane, air, and absorber remains the same as those used
for the cylindrical model. The ambient air is again modeled as a sphere with all other
volumes subtracted from it, however since the outer geometry dimensions differ from
the cylindrical microphone, ra is increased by 50 % to 3.4 mm. A representative plot of
the model is shown in Figure 4.20.

is the membrane.

microphone recording range, no further effort is performed to assess the issue.
The rectangular model also seems to have the same discrepancy, whether it is angled or
not. This matter is further explored in Section 4.5.
4.5
Viewing Results with a Numerical Calibration
As brieﬂy hinted in the comments given to Figures 4.19 and 4.24, applications of numer-
ical calibration for the simulated responses may clarify how well the numerical model’s
tendencies match those of the experimental. Figures 4.25 and 4.26 show how applica-
tions of +32 dB SPL for the Sonion 8002 microphone and +43 dB SPL for the Sonion
50GC31 microphone yield responses very near the experimental ones. Note that this
numerical calibration is mathematically incorrect because [dB SPL] is a logarithmic unit.
Adding +32 dB SPL to 30 dB SPL is not the same as adding it to 20 dB SPL, not to men-
tion the wrongness in even adding two values of [dB SPL] together. It is nevertheless
included here simply to help show the tendencies of the graphs.
68
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
(A) The membrane response when the microphone is mounted on its bottom.
(B) The membrane response when the microphone is mounted on its side.
FIGURE 4.25: Cylindrical Sonion 8002 electret condenser microphone.
For the cylindrical model, the numerical calibration makes it clear that there is an agree-
ment in tendencies. The discrepancies that are visible for frequencies ≳9 kHz are likely
to be caused by the neglected dissipative effects of the air gap boundary layer as also men-
tioned in Section 4.2.3, recall that the ﬁrst eigenfrequency of the membrane is 16.59 kHz,
see Table 2.3.

For the rectangular model, the tendency agreement is even more clear.
4.6
Using the Numerical Microphone Models
The tendency agreements between experiments and simulations enable the numerical
models to foresee how the microphones are affected by vibrations, albeit with incorrect
magnitudes. Since the numerical models can simulate much more controlled cyclic
load cases, the effects of each isolated input can be used to shed light on the vibration
sensitivity properties of the microphones. Here it is of interest which frequency and
direction of mechanically excited vibrations, cause the largest diaphragm responses.
70
==PAGE:86==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments

the cylindrical microphone model is now solved for a constant amplitude of ¯dAmp =
8.1758 × 10−10 m and a scaling factor of Sd = 100, in varying directions. The results are
plotted in Figure 4.27.

The results look counterintuitive in the sense that axial excitation does not yield the
largest membrane displacements. To investigate this phenomenon, snapshots of the
pressure ﬁeld of each curve are presented in Figure 4.30:
(A) Input in z (↕).
(B) Input in x (↔).
(C) Input in zx (↕↔).
(D) Input in y (↔), Note that the micro-
phone is turned 90° around z in the plot.3
FIGURE 4.30: Pressure ﬁeld snapshots of the rectangular model for different excitation
inputs, all at Ω= 10 kHz. The color bar displays pressure differences ∆p [Pa].
Figures 4.30a and 4.30b look very similar, however the diaphragm deﬂection on Fig-
ure 4.30b is slightly larger. The large diaphragm response can be explained by the fact
that the direction of excitation is perpendicular to the plane of the inlet hole. So when
the microphone is displaced in this direction, air is drawn in, affecting the diaphragm.
3Since the microphone model is meshed freely over the yz-plane, the element edges (black lines)
become irregular and numerous in this plot.
73
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 4. Finite Element Model of Microphone-on-Rod-Experiments
This effect yields a larger response than shaking the microphone perpendicular to the
diaphragm plane. Figure 4.30c hints that when the two effects are superposed, they
reduce each other. The diaphragm responds like it is excited in the second mode. But
since the ’right-most’ bend of the diaphragm has a higher amplitude than the ’left-most’
part, it is unlikely to be a mode, but rather the effects of the position of the inlet, causing
air pressure deviations. This questions the use of the middle node probe as the repre-
sentative membrane response, but the error is not easily countered since the converter
(Section 4.1.5) also relies on the middle node displacement. From Figure 4.30d, when
exciting in the y-direction, the diaphragm is hardly affected.
It may also be of interest to learn more about how the microphone responds to ’tilting’
vibrations, as a hearing aid microphone presumably also can be exposed to. ’Tilting’
excitation is modeled by displacing two of the bottom edge nodes of the microphone
anti-symmetrically in z. Since the cylindrical microphone is axisymmetric, this has only
been performed around the x-axis, while the rectangular microphone is tilted around its
x- and y-axes separately. Figure 4.31 shows the results for all microphone models tilted
at a constant amplitude of ¯dAmp = 8.1758/2 × 10−10 m.

==PAGE:90==
CHAPTER
FIVE
DISCUSSIONS, CONCLUSIONS, AND FURTHER WORK
In this chapter, sub-conclusions from Chapters 2 to 4 are summarized, critically discussed,
and applied in order to ultimately address the objectives stated in Section 1.1. This is
followed by a section with thoughts and ideas on further work.
5.1
Discussion
5.1.1
The Mathematical Models
The analyses performed in Chapter 2 serve as a mathematical introduction to the behav-
ior of membranes, and the foundation for the expanded numerical models of Chapter 4.
The diaphragm is analytically modeled as a mechanical membrane constrained in all
DOF along its edge. The obtained formulae of the rectangular- and circular membrane
dynamics are used to evaluate the same membrane models built in ANSYS. For the rect-
angluar membrane, eigenvalues and frequency response functions are compared and no
signiﬁcant discrepancies are found, see Sections 2.1.1 and 2.1.2. The frequency response
function gets more inaccurate at the higher excitation frequencies, but as shown from
the mesh convergence analysis in Section 4.4.1, it can beneﬁt from being solved with a
ﬁner mesh. In the working frequency range of the microphones; Ω= [0.1; 10] kHz, the
results match very well. The circular membrane is validated against an analytical static
solution and eigenfrequencies, also with no signiﬁcant discrepancies. However, already
in the case of just a membrane with a ﬁxed boundary, it is necessary in ANSYS to apply
75
==PAGE:91==
Chapter 5. Discussions, Conclusions and Further Work
some modeling contortions. The ﬁrst one being the application of the membrane pre-
tension, P, applied by a tweak of temperature with an arbitrary temperature expansion
coefﬁcient αmem. On its own, this trick works ﬁne, and the stress in the membrane plane
matches the pre-tension perfectly. But when solved by harmonic analysis in ANSYS,
all forces, displacements, and even temperature gradients are applied with sinusoidal
varying magnitude. The attempts to overcome this challenge have been numerous, but
in all cases no solution was found, or an even bigger issue created. With the analytical
and numerical model matching each other, the problem is insigniﬁcant, at least for the
isolated membrane models, but this is only so because the applied uniform pressure is
dominant, compared to the cyclic membrane pre-tension.
5.1.2
The Experiments
In Chapter 3, three experimental setups are initially used to determine the optimum rod
design. The best rod is the one with the most z-dominant and controlled (i.e. minimizing
rod eigenfrequency disturbances) vibrations, while still having sufﬁcient length to render
the sound pressure emitted from the shaker negligible. Of the two proposed steel rod
designs, illustrated in Figure 3.5, the 5.0 cm conical design is chosen. As mentioned
in the short summary in Section 3.3, this design yields the best compromise between
controlled vibrations and distance to the shaker.
The 5.0 cm conical rod yields dominant axial vibration amplitudes of the microphone
housing for Ω≲2 kHz, from there the transverse vibration amplitudes are in the same
order as the axial (see Section 3.2.5). The reasons behind the large transverse amplitudes
are tested through various tests. From Sections 3.2.2 and 3.2.4, it is concluded that the
bending eigenfrequencies and the transverse movement of the shaker’s moving mass are
the two factors most responsible for the unwanted transverse vibrations, thus a different
type of shaker that dampens transverse vibrations is desirable. From Section 3.2.3, the
effects of the glue and beeswax adhesive assembly materials can be safely neglected.
The microphone displacement is measured in the x-, y- and z directions, to be used as
input excitation in ANSYS, including phase angles. But since the LDV equipment only
has a single laser, simultaneous measurements in different directions are difﬁcult to
obtain. One suggested, and tested, solution of 45° tilted mirrors yields too noisy data
because of scattering. Instead the laser is moved around the test specimen (shaker, rod
and microphone), to capture the displacement in each direction, one at a time. This
requires the assumption that the test specimen acts the same way between different tests,
which has not been investigated. A triple-axis LDV setup is desirable to avoid this issue.
76
==PAGE:92==
Chapter 5. Discussions, Conclusions and Further Work
The anechoic chamber tests are together with the holography tests used to help verify if
the distance from the shaker to the microphone is sufﬁciently long to avoid the acoustic
radiation from the shaker’s bafﬂe. Once the rod design is concluded, the anechoic cham-
ber tests are used to approach the isolated mechanical vibration response amplitudes,
which is achieved by subtracting the response of a still control microphone from that of a
shaken microphone. Intuitively the results seem ﬁne, but they are difﬁcult to verify. From
Figure 3.19, the cylindrical microphone has a near constant response of ∼65 dB SPL,
while the rectangular microphone response is much more inconsistent, and with a large
peak around Ω∼8 kHz visible in Figure 3.20. Having completely different geometries,
the differences between their responses is simply accepted. Angling the microphone
90° produces results of signiﬁcantly lower amplitude up to ∼6 kHz, clearly marking
that axial vibrations are dominant for lower frequencies. One drawback of the anechoic
chamber tests is that in order to minimize the external sound, and thus get as pure a
response as possible, the test specimen is encapsulated. This renders it impossible to use
the LDV to measure the microphone motions while testing. So an assumption that the
microphone responds the same way in the anechoic chamber as when performing LDV
tests is necessary. Some errors are thus to be expected by small scale boundary condition
changes from moving the shaker and rod in between experiment setups.
5.1.3
The Numerical Models
Chapter 4 presents numerical models coded in the APDL-language of ANSYS, conver-
gence studies, and their validations by experimentally obtained responses.
A few ANSYS-speciﬁc complications requires the development and application of some
tricks. The membrane cannot be modeled in one layer, as the ﬂuid elements attached to
both sides are able to transfer pressure right through it, with no interaction occurring.
Thus two layers are required, complicating the creation of especially the 3D models.
Once implemented, and with each layer having half of the resulting stiffness and density
desired, the models work exactly as the one-layered membranes when no air is present,
see Section 4.1.2. When advancing to 3D, air-to-air elements proved to work incorrectly
when disabling FSI (KEYOPT(2)=1) for the FLUID30 element type, necessitating the use
of expensive 4 DOF (x-, y-, and z displacement and pressure) air elements, increasing
computational time substantially. Finally, the problem of the unwanted sine-varying
in-plane membrane pre-tension mentioned earlier proves to have a non-negligible effect,
when displacement amplitudes become as low as ∼10−10 m (from LDV experiments,
see Figure 4.1). To counter this, all displacement inputs are multiplied by a scaling
factor of Sd = 100, and the responses are afterwards divided by the same scaling factor.
Section 4.1.4 covers this thoroughly.
77
==PAGE:93==
Chapter 5. Discussions, Conclusions and Further Work
ANSYS speciﬁc issues are not the only limitations to the numerical models. In order to
emphasize the important features of the microphones, and avoid mesh complications, all
non-diaphragm interior components of the microphones are neglected. The backplate,
that gives a small air gap in the order of ∼20 µm to the diaphragm, can be troublesome
to neglect. 20 µm is about the size of the ﬂuid-structure boundary layer where effects
of viscous and thermal damping become apparent, thus affecting the diaphragm dis-
placement, [19]. The effects are negligible for small excitation frequencies, since the
backplate design is tuned to give the largest dampening effect around the ﬁrst diaphragm
eigenfrequency. The error caused by this assumption is thus expected to be most evident
for higher frequency ranges, probably causing the numerical model to yield too large
response amplitudes at higher values of Ω. From the ﬁgures presented in Section 4.5
this seems to be the case for Ω≳9 kHz for the cylindrical microphone. The thickness
of the air gap would require the mesh to be impractically ﬁne, therefore the backplate
is omitted. Converting results from microphone outputs in [dB V] to ANSYS outputs in
[m] is not trivial. An artiﬁcial converter is introduced (see Section 4.1.5), converting the
microphone outputs to [dB SPL], to [Pa], and then calculate what membrane center dis-
placement ANSYS- and analytical models give when exposed to such pressure. Though
the analytical- and numerical models agree, there is no experimental data or method
available to validate them by.
The numerical models are tested for mesh- and bubble size convergence, and coherency
between axisymmetric- and 3D cylindrical model responses. All yield good results with
reasonable meshes and bubble sizes, except for the rectangular model that is close to
convergence, but ﬁnally meshed with a compromising ﬁneness, in order to cope with
simulation-time.
Using a 2D axisymmetric- and a 3D model, the cylindrical microphone’s response is simu-
lated based on LDV displacement inputs. The simulated responses match the tendencies
of the experimental results from Section 3.2.7, but differ substantially in magnitude. The
problems and limitations stated further above may be some of the reasons behind the
large discrepancies.
Although the magnitude of the numerical results are unreliable, their correct tendencies
still enable them to ﬁnd the direction of vibrations that cause the largest diaphragm mid-
point response. The cylindrical microphone model is solved for a constant amplitude of
vibrations in z, x, and zx respectively, see Section 4.6. It is clear that z-directional ampli-
tudes by far dominate those in the x-direction. But also that higher excitation frequencies
cause larger diaphragm responses for z-displacement amplitudes. When performing the
same tests on the rectangular model, some interesting results apply. Shaking the mi-
crophone in the direction normal to its inlet (x-direction), causes a larger diaphragm
78
==PAGE:94==
Chapter 5. Discussions, Conclusions and Further Work
center response compared to shaking the microphone normal to the diaphragm plane.
The pressure plots in Figure 4.30 also reveal that the diaphragm does not displace in its
ﬁrst mode, but closer to its second, resembling an uneven ’S’-form. This questions the
applicability of always using the membrane center node as response reference, but is
necessary due to the nature of the converter, that is also reliant on the membrane cen-
ter node deﬂection. From the plots of ’tilted’ responses in Figure 4.31, the rectangular
microphone experiences an increasing – and the cylindrical a decreasing – response for
higher excitation frequencies. The rectangular microphone is much more affected by
’tilting’ vibrations than the cylindrical.
79
==PAGE:95==
Chapter 5. Discussions, Conclusions and Further Work
5.2
Conclusions
By numerous experiments, data treatments, and numerical simulations, the following
conclusions are obtained in reference to the objectives listed in Section 1.1:
1. A 5.0 cm conical steel rod is found to give the best compromise between controlled
mechanical vibrations and sufﬁcient distance between the microphone and the
sound emitting shaker. With a conical shape, the rod is able to support the mi-
crophone with no sharp-edge transitions, while also having a high stiffness and
thereby attain high eigenfrequencies. The conical shape also beneﬁts from a rel-
atively large weight that lowers the amplitudes of the unwanted sound pressure
waves emitted from the shaker.
2. Using the 5.0 cm conical rod as mounting device, the vibration amplitudes and
phase angles of the Sonion 8002 microphone housing in z-, x- and y directions are
recorded for excitation frequencies ranging [0.1; 10] kHz. The transverse vibrations
show ineligible behavior for ≳2 kHz. The bending eigenfrequencies of the rod,
and unsteadiness in the shaker’s moving mass are found to be the causes of this.
3. The isolated mechanically induced vibration response of the diaphragm is found to
be relatively ﬂat and near ∼65 dB SPL for the Sonion 8002 microphone. Angling
it 90° lowers the response signiﬁcantly for excitation frequencies ≲7 kHz. However,
for higher frequencies, the response increases, also proving the unavoidable sig-
niﬁcance of transverse vibrations. The Sonion 50GC31 microphone shows similar
behavior, albeit with a more unsteady response.
4. When exciting the numerical models with amplitude- and phase data obtained
by LDV experiments, the simulated diaphragm responses show tendencies that
match those of experimental tests in the anechoic chamber. Both the rectangular-
and cylindrical microphone models show large discrepancies in response magni-
tudes. This means that the models can foresee tendencies, and be used to compare
responses for different load cases, but that magnitudes are underestimated.
5. Through numerical simulations, the cylindrical microphone is found to be dom-
inated by z-directional excitation, and almost unresponsive to excitations in the
x-direction. The rectangular microphone is however more affected by x- than
z directional excitation, but when superimposed the resulting effects cancel out,
yielding a signiﬁcantly lower response than if excited solely in any of the two direc-
tions. The response for the y-directional excitation is found to be negligible for the
rectangular microphone. When ’tilted’, the rectangular microphone yields a larger
response than the cylindrical.
80
==PAGE:96==
5.3
In Reference to Hearing Aids
Recalling the motivation of feedback reduction discussed in Section 1.3, the numerical
models documented in Chapter 4 can be used to run tests and learn about the signiﬁ-
cance and roles of certain cyclic mechanical load cases on the microphone housing. For
example, the angling of the microphone matters when considering structural vibrations
of the hearing aid shell. If assumed that the largest shell vibration amplitudes occur
normal to the shell, it can be concluded from Section 4.6 that having an inlet in the
direction normal to the shell will cause the largest membrane displacements.
Other microphone models, but in terms of assumptions still similar to the Sonion 8002
and 50GC31, can be modeled by the same principles as described in Chapter 4, and be
expected to yield results that resemble tendencies correctly. This can prove valuable
when designing new hearing aids with other microphones being considered.
5.4
Further Work
This section gives the authors’ ideas and wishes for further work on the subject of the
project, that are out-of-scope in the present thesis:
• Find a way to obtain the diaphragm displacement experimentally. The artiﬁcially
created converter is suspected to hold a source of error which could be avoided
this way. One idea is to investigate the possibility of coating the diaphragm with a
material, without affecting the diaphragm response, while still enabling the laser to
measure on it. For this, a very small laser dot radius is required, and the assumption
that the membrane only vibrates in its ﬁrst mode.
• Model the microphones in other ﬁnite element software packages such as Comsol
or Abaqus.
This can validate the ANSYS models further, and test if the large
discrepancies between experiments and simulations become smaller.
• Investigate the possibility of creating a test setup that eliminates the transverse
vibrations. This could be by another rod design, a different shaker, or perhaps a
completely new test setup, using another way to shake and measure the micro-
phone. If this is possible, then geometrically axisymmetric microphones can be
modeled and simulated for larger geometric features such as a rod, inlet tube, etc.
• Expand the ﬁnite element model to include the backplate and other microphone in-
terior features. Try to test what effects an air ﬁlled tube (spout) on the microphone
inlet has on the diaphragm response.
